[1] One - Tomb
[2] The tone of the Case of Kaiser was gloomy from the beginning. It started with the death of an elderly man, which turned out to be very appropriate in retrospect. The death of this elderly man, like counterpoint music, was perfectly in sync with the intricate rhythm of the funeral march that followed, lacking the mournful melodies of a requiem mass. The orchestra concluded with a strong, sinister sound, and the requiem continued to echo in the ears of New Yorkers long after the final, ominous note had faded away.
[3] It goes without saying that when Jody Kagarice died of heart failure, no one would have thought that it was the overture to a symphony of murder. Ellery Queen certainly did not think so. In fact, it can be inferred that Ellery Queen learned of Jody Kagarice's death three days after the old blind man's body was buried in the most normal way possible, in a place that everyone thought was his final resting place, only then did it catch his attention.
[4] When the newspapers first reported the death of Carstairs - Ellery was not a regular reader of newspapers or magazines, so he did not see the obituary - they did not pay attention to the fact that the location of the grave had a story to tell. Only an interesting article about it was published in the old New York Magazine. Carstairs' decaying brownstone building at 111 East 54th Street, next to an old church with its front door facing Fifth Avenue, occupies half of the land between Fifth Avenue and Madison Avenue, with the church's north side bordering Fifty-fifth Street and its south side bordering Fifty-fourth Street. The cemetery between Carstairs' house and the main body of the church is one of the oldest private cemeteries in the city. The deceased's remains were buried in this cemetery. The Carstairs family, as parishioners of the church, had been there for almost two hundred years, exempt from health laws that prohibited burial in the city center. They were allowed to rest under the shadow of the skyscrapers on Fifth Avenue because they had always owned an underground ossuary in the church cemetery - a place where bones were stored underground, hidden from passersby as the grave openings were three feet below ground level, and no tombstones were visible on the grass of the church cemetery.
[5] The funeral was quiet and uneventful, without tears or fanfare. The body was anointed with embalming oil, dressed in evening attire, and placed in a glossy black casket, resting on a catafalque in the ground floor living room of the Carstairs' residence. The funeral service was conducted by John Henry Adda, the pastor from the neighboring church - a notable mention is that the sermons and humorous writings of this Adda pastor were regularly featured in prominent positions of the diocesan newspaper. There were no dramatic scenes or hysterics, only a brief and characteristic fainting spell by the deceased's housekeeper, Mrs. Sims.
[6] However, Joan Blythe later recounted that there was something amiss. We might consider this as a result of the superior performance of female intuition, which the medical community often dismissed as sheer nonsense. Regardless, she stiffened her face and spoke in a peculiarly British accent, describing the "tense atmosphere." Who caused the tension, and who was responsible for it – if there was indeed tension – she could not say, or perhaps did not want to. On the contrary, everything seemed to be in order, with a touch of understated melancholy. For example, after the simple ceremony ended, the family members, along with scattered friends and servants, formed a procession to pass by the casket one last time to pay their respects, before returning to their seats with solemn dignity. Tiffany, looking pale and drawn, wept, but her tears were graceful – a single drop, a gentle wipe, a sigh. Dimitriou, whom everyone called "the idiot," stared blankly, as if mesmerized by the flat, unfeeling countenance of his cousin in the casket.
[7] Gilbert Sloan patted his wife's plump hand. Allen Cheneau blushed slightly, his hands in the pockets of his coat, glaring up at the ceiling. The director of the Kakistos Art Gallery, Natchio Suicca, stood listlessly in a corner, impeccably dressed for the occasion. The deceased's legal representative, Wu Zhongfu, sniffled audibly. Everything was so natural, so flawless. Thus, the undertaker named Stuart, with his furrowed brow and efficient demeanor, handled the body and nailed the coffin lid shut. There was nothing left but the tedious ritual of walking around the coffin one last time. Allen, Dammy, Sloan, and Suicca stood on either side of the coffin, waiting for the ritualistic procession to settle down before they lifted the coffin onto their shoulders, with the undertaker Stuart carefully attending to them. Ada the priest murmured a prayer, and the group then steadily walked out of the residence.
[8] Ellery Queen later positively said that Joan Bret was a very careful young lady. If she felt "tension in the air," then there was indeed tension in the air. However, where did it come from? It was difficult to pinpoint exactly - from whom. Perhaps it was from Dr. Wotz, with the big beard, who stood at the end of the line with Mrs. Frieland. Perhaps it was from the men carrying the coffin, or from the people who followed Joan. In fact, it could have come from the house itself, due to the tension created by the West
[9] Mrs. Muspratt was sobbing hysterically on her bed, or else, due to the simple circumstances such as Mr. Wiggs, the butler, stroking his own chin in the dead man's study.
[10] This, of course, did not present any obstacle to the funeral procession. The group of people did not enter the fifth-fourth street from the front gate, but entered the long courtyard from the back gate, which was surrounded by six households on the fifth-fourth street and the fifth-fifth street, becoming a private alley for these six households. The group turned left and walked through the door on the right side of the courtyard, arriving at the cemetery. Passersby and people who were attracted to the fifth-fourth street like flies to see the spectacle may have felt cheated, as choosing this non-public route to the cemetery was merely to make them feel cheated. Everyone stood close to the spiked fence, peering through the iron bars at the small cemetery; among the crowd were journalists and photographers, all of whom were unusually quiet.
[11] The actors in the tragedy were indifferent to the audience. The group crossed the bare grass and joined another small group of people who were gathered around a rectangular patch of grass - Stewarts' assistant - and the church sexton, Henriwell; as well as an old lady wearing a bizarre, outdated black hat, wiping her swollen, mucous membranes with a handkerchief.
[12] If we trust Joan Baez's intuition, then tension still exists.
[13] However, everything that followed was as flawless as everything that had come before. The old-fashioned rituals and ceremonies; a gravedigger bent forward, tightly gripping the rusted iron handle of the old door embedded in the ground; lifeless; the coffin slowly descended into the old-bricked tomb; the workers turned, uttering a few low and hurried syllables, and the coffin gradually moved to one side, disappearing as it entered one of the many niches in the underground ossuary, the iron door clanged shut, covered with earth and turf…
[14] For some reason, Joanne Bright later spoke with certainty about her impression of the situation at the time, saying that the tension in the atmosphere somehow did not exist.
[15] Searching
[16] Only during the brief period when the funeral procession returned to the house from the back garden did the tense atmosphere cease to exist.
[17] It reappeared, followed by a series of mysterious events. It would take a long time before the cause of these events would become clear.
[18] The first sign of trouble was the outcry of the deceased's lawyer's representative, Maurice Wutherspoon. The situation became clear and obvious. The vicar, Mr. Fawcett, had returned to the Casgarth home to console the bereaved, followed by the church sexton, Henneville, who was short and nimble in handling church matters, constantly moving around. The old lady with watery eyes and mucous membranes, who met the group at the cemetery, naturally followed the entire group back, now also in the living room, examining the empty coffin with a critical gaze. The undertaker, Stuart, was busy with his assistants, dismantling the eerie scene they had set up earlier. No one had invited the old lady in; no one had noticed her presence, except perhaps the oddball Dumbshow, who looked at her with a seemingly rational gaze of disgust. The rest were either sitting down or lazily walking around; few spoke; besides the undertaker and his assistants, it seemed that everyone was idle.
[19] Maurice Wutherspoon, like everyone else, was restless, trying to pass the time after the burial. He walked into the deceased's study, where he claimed to be aimlessly wandering. The servant, Weggis, quickly stood up, as if he had just dozed off. Wutherspoon waved his hand and continued aimlessly walking through the room, heading towards a wall between two bookcases where Casgarth's safe was embedded. Wutherspoon firmly claimed that he had moved the safe's number dial, selected a combination of numbers, and opened the thick circular door purely as a mechanical action. He later asserted that he had not intended to look for it and had not imagined that it would be lost. In fact, just five minutes before the funeral procession left the house, he had seen it and even touched it! However, the fact was that Wutherspoon had indeed discovered it, along with the iron box that contained it, had disappeared; whether this was due to chance or
[20] - event - this discovery, which became a warning signal, caused the tension to reappear, followed by a series of terrible events.
[21] "How long have you been sitting here?"
[22] "Sir, I have been sitting here since the funeral procession left for the cemetery."
[23] "Have any other people come into the house while you were sitting here?"
[24] "Sir, no one has come into the house while I was sitting here."

[29] When these two men entered with puzzled expressions, Wu Zhuo was standing in front of the safe with a posture of a savior.

As for Aren, the boy remained as gloomy as ever, and Wu Zhuo the lawyer could smell the strong whiskey on his breath. Wu Zhuo didn't have time to dilly-dally. He pointed to the open safe with a highly suspicious gaze. Shiloan shook his lion-like head; he was well-dressed, confident, and impeccably stylish. Aren remained silent – he shrugged his bony shoulders and showed no reaction.
[31] "Well," said Wutherspoon, "there's no loss to me. But, gentlemen, I'm determined to get to the bottom of this. Let's proceed."
[32] Wutherspoon seemed quite pleased with himself. Everyone in the house had to listen to his call, and they all gathered in the study. It seemed strange, but it was true, that the funeral procession had returned to the Kestrel house less than four minutes ago, and had already been summoned by Wutherspoon to this very carpet - everyone, even the undertaker, Stuart, and his assistant! - men, women, and children, all denied taking anything from the safe, and even claimed they hadn't been near the safe that day. Wutherspoon could only take their word for it with a doubtful expression.
[33] In such a dramatic and somewhat comical moment, both Joan Bryant and Alan Chene had the same thought. They both rushed out the door, pushing and shoving each other to the hall, then from the hall into the corridor. Wutherspoon called out, following them closely, not knowing what was going on. Alan and Joan managed to open the door to the corridor, then pushed through the entrance hall, and opened the unlocked front door that faced the street. They faced the slightly surprised crowd outside, with Wutherspoon following closely behind them.
[34] At this point, Joan spoke in a clear female voice: "Who has come to this house in the last half hour?"
[35] Alan also shouted, "Who?"
[36] Wu Zhuo couldn't help repeating the word. A group of reporters were peering at the closed door on the sidewalk, and one of the young men answered: "No one has come!"
[37] Another reporter said calmly: "What's going on, old man? Why won't you let us in? We won't get in the way."
[38] Some people in the crowd on the street also expressed the same sentiment. Joan blushed naturally and brushed her brown hair, unconsciously adjusting her hairline. Alan shouted again: "Has anyone come out?"
[39] - A thunderous roar answered him: "No!"
[40] Wu Zhuo coughed a few times, and the crowd scene weakened his confidence. He angrily chased the young couple back into the house and carefully locked the door - this time, both doors were locked. However, Wu Zhuo's self-confidence would not be shaken for long. As soon as he entered the study, he regained his confidence. Everyone in the room, sitting or standing, was bewildered. He asked them questions one after another, and when he found that almost everyone in the family knew the combination of the safe, he was almost shouting with disappointment. "Well," he said, "well. Someone is up to something. Someone is lying. But we'll soon find out. Soon, I dare say." He paced back and forth in front of them, "I'll be frank with you. It's my responsibility - my responsibility, you should understand," everyone nodded at him, like a set of dolls nodding, "I'm going to search everyone in this house. Let's get started right away." Everyone stopped nodding, "I know someone here doesn't like this idea. Do you think I like it? But I have to do it. It was stolen right under my nose. My nose." When he said this, regardless of how solemn the scene was, Joan Bright chuckled; Wu Zhuo's nose indeed occupied a large area on his face.

[44] "Illusion? Is that your opinion?" Wu Zhuo Fu said, "When we find a thief among you, you'll know it's not my illusion."

[49] The third mystery

[51] Mr. Pepper, the deputy prosecutor, was a charming young man. Half an hour after Watanabe called the police, he arrived at the Kakizaki house, and from that moment on, things went smoothly. He had the gift of making people talk because he knew the value of flattery - a skill that Watanabe, the poor lawyer, did not possess. To Watanabe's surprise, he felt more comfortable after a brief conversation with Pepper. No one noticed the fat, round-faced man with a cigar in his mouth who accompanied Pepper - a man named Hesse, an investigator from the district attorney's office; Hesse stood quietly by the door to the study, smoking without attracting attention, as instructed by Pepper.
[52] Watanabe pulled Pepper aside and recounted the events of the funeral.
[53] "Well, that's the situation, Pepper. Just five minutes before the funeral procession lined up in this house, I went into Kakizaki's bedroom" - he vaguely indicated the other door that led to the study - "took the key to Kakizaki's iron box, came back here, opened the safe, opened the iron box, and I clearly saw it was still inside. But now -"
[54] "What was inside?"
[55] "Didn't I tell you? I must have been too excited." Pepper didn't need to point out to Watanabe that he was too excited, as he wiped the sweat from his face, "I'm talking about Kakizaki's new will, a new one, please note! The iron box contained the new will, and that's for sure; I picked it up and looked at it, and it had my own seal on it. I put it back in the box, locked the box, locked the safe, and left the room..."
[56] "Wait a minute, Mr. Wu." - Mr. Pepper had a method to his madness, he always addressed those who might lead to clues as "Mr." - "Is there anyone else who has the key to the box?"
[57] "Absolutely not, Mr. Pepper, absolutely not! It's the only key to the box, and Mr. Kagisu recently told me so; I found it in his pocket while I was in his bedroom, and after I locked the box and the safe, I put the key in my own pocket. In fact, it's on my own keychain. It's still there."
[58] Mr. Wu took out a key ring from his hip pocket and picked out a small key with trembling fingers, unclasping it and handing it to Mr. Pepper.
[59] "I can guarantee it's been in my pocket all this time. Oh, it's impossible for someone to steal it from me!" - Mr. Pepper nodded solemnly - "There's no time for that either. Just as I left the study, we started arranging the procession, and then we attended the funeral. When we came back, for some reason, I came here again, opened the safe - oh, God, the will and the box are all gone!"
[60] Mr. Pepper clicked his tongue sympathetically: "Who do you think took it?"
[61] "Estimate?" Wu Zhuo looked around the room. "I have enough estimates, but I'm sorry, there's no evidence! Listen, Pepper. The situation is clear. First point: when I saw the will in the box, everyone in this house is still here
[62] In the house; no one ever went out and did not return. Second point: All those who attended the funeral procession left the house in a row, passed through the backyard to the cemetery, and their entire time at the cemetery can be verified, and they did not come into contact with any other outsiders except for the few they encountered there. Third point: When the original group returned to the house, these few outsiders came along with them, and they are still here.”
[63] "This is quite an interesting situation," said Pepper sharply. "In other words, if one of the original group stole the will and passed it on to this outsider, it would be pointless, as they would be exposed when searched, unless the will was hidden along the way or in the cemetery. Very interesting, Mr. Wu. So, who are these outsiders?"
[64] Wu Zhuofu pointed to the old lady with the outdated black hat: "She is one of them. This old hag is Mrs. Susan Moth, who lives in one of the six houses surrounding the backyard. So she is a neighbor." Pepper nodded, and Wu Zhuofu pointed to the church sexton who was trembling behind Ada's pastor, "And this little man, Henriwell, is the sexton of the neighboring church; the two workers beside him are gravediggers, hired by the man over there - that man is the undertaker, Mr. Stuart. Now, the fourth point: no one came or went during our time in the cemetery - I confirmed this with the reporters waiting outside. And after confirming that no one came or went, I locked all the doors myself, so no one could get in or out anymore."

[202] Chapter Five
[203] On Thursday, October 7, an ordinary day, Erle Simpson held a war council. It was on this day that Ellery Queen came into contact with the complicated riddle that would later be known as the Kaskist Case. At this point, Ellery was still young and arrogant, and his relationship with the New York City police was not yet fully established, so he was a bit of a gate-crasher, even though he was the son of Richard Quin, the police officer. In fact, even the seasoned officer had doubts about Ellery's method of solving actual criminal cases by relying solely on pure reasoning. However, since Ellery had successfully handled a few isolated cases using his developing deductive skills, this served as a precedent, allowing him to confidently judge that he would be attending the meeting when Simpson rang the opening bell.
[204] To be honest, Ellery had no idea about George Kaskist's death or the theft of the will.
[205] Therefore, the questions he posed to the prosecutor were things that everyone else at the meeting knew without asking. At this point, the prosecutor was not yet as close to Ellery as he would be in later years, and he showed impatience, which made the officer angry and made it clear. Ellery leaned back in Simpson's high-quality leather chair, his face slightly flushed.
[206] The attendees were all serious. There was Simpson, who was still relatively new as a prosecutor and had a small, wiry build but exuded strength and determination - sharp-eyed and alert, he did not immediately delve into the seemingly absurd problem at hand. There was Pepper, a sharp-witted member of Simpson's prosecution team in charge of administrative work, whose entire muscular frame now exuded disappointment. There was also Crowning, Simpson's first assistant prosecutor, who was much more mature and wise in handling criminal cases than his two colleagues; he was an old hand - with red hair, a bit neurotic, quick as a colt, and experienced as a stallion. There was also Detective Richard Queen, who was old and shriveled, with a small, pointed face and gray hair, looking more like a bird than a man - this wiry old man had a penchant for old-fashioned ties, possessed a latent elasticity like a hound, and had extensive knowledge of traditional criminal investigation methods. He was now vigorously playing with his brown snuffbox, which he had carried with him for years.
[207] Of course, there was Ellery - at this point, he was still unpolished. When he made a point, he waved his shiny spectacles. When he smiled, his entire face radiated joy - a handsome face with a long, chiseled profile that was both attractive and endearing, with clear, bright eyes that showed his intelligence. Otherwise, he was no different from any other young man just out of school: tall, unkempt, broad-shouldered, and athletic. As he looked at Inspector Simpson, the latter felt distinctly uncomfortable.
[208] "Well, folks, we're back to square one," Simpson grumbled. "We've got a lot of leads, but nowhere to go. Well, Pepper, have you found anything else to keep us on our toes?"
[209] "Nothing important," Pepper answered gloomily. "I naturally took the opportunity to grill Sloan - I had a private chat with him. He's the only one who stands to lose out from Cardis' new will. But Sloan clammed up all day yesterday. What can I do? We have no evidence."
[210] "There's always a way," the detective muttered vaguely.

[212] "Well, Willy and I have reached a dead end, and we both know it. We don't have the authority to isolate the house, so Willy had to send two of his men away yesterday. I was reluctant to give up so easily, so I stayed there all night last night - I don't think they knew I was there."
[213] "Did you find anything?" Crooning asked curiously.

[748] Ten: Presages
[749] At this point, Ehlerie Quine was a curious little troublemaker. In these few hours, he had experienced a series of sudden inspirations –
[750] – and felt a vague longing for what lay before him – as if he were in a dreamlike state that he could not quite grasp. However, he had a gut feeling that a breakthrough was imminent. He wandered around the study, bumping into people, messing around with furniture and books, and generally causing a nuisance. He passed by the shelf with the filter jug twice, sniffing the air – not out of any specific smell, but rather out of a faint, unpleasant odor. He furrowed his brows and looked at it, then lifted the lid to take a peek inside. Whatever he hoped to find, there was nothing out of the ordinary; all he saw was water.
[751] Even so, when he looked up, there was a gleam of hope in his eyes, and he whistled a tune as he thought, which infuriated his father. The question the detective had asked was, of course, not going to be answered; instead, Ellery took his natural sharp tongue and turned it on Mrs. Sims.
[752] "Where were the shelf and the tea set when you found Mr. Cagney dead on Saturday morning?"

Each of the three saucers held a yellowed, used tea bag and a dried, pressed lemon. The policeman and Pepper saw no more than this.

[895] He grinned. "There's a lot of evidence, but making an accurate conclusion comes with a bit of risk. The body's heat dissipation, coupled with the muscle changes and the overall discoloration to a bluish-gray, suggests that it's been around seven days. The discoloration caused by chemical and bacterial action on the body's surface and abdominal center is quite apparent: the number and location of internal and external bluish-gray decay spots indicate a seven-day gap since yesterday. The compressed gas, the mucus removed from the nose and mouth, the rot in the trachea, and certain signs in the intestines and stomach - all point to a seven-day period. The skin is tight, but in the most swollen area - the abdomen - it has begun to loosen. The smell, the reduced weight - yes, I can say that Mr. Arpège Greene was killed six and a half days ago, before the coffin was opened yesterday morning."

[1797] Twenty: Scheming
[1798] The Cajolus Collection, located on Madison Avenue, was dark and quiet that night when Inspector Quin, Erlenmeyer Quin, and Inspector Willi led a group of detectives in a surprise attack from all directions. They moved with quiet efficiency, and the only sound was the click of their footsteps on the pavement. The windows of the large display case were dark, and the doors were locked and guarded by an electric fence. However, a side entrance caught their attention, and the officers whispered to each other. Inspector Willi pressed a button marked "night bell," and they waited silently. There was no response, so Willi pressed the bell again. Five minutes passed with no sound or light, and Willi sighed and motioned for his men to push the door. The door creaked and clanked as it opened, and they stumbled into a pitch-black hallway.
[1799] They descended a staircase and came to another door, which had a security alarm system. They forcefully pushed the door open without concern for triggering an alarm, revealing a long, dark display room filled with portraits on the walls, precious artifacts in glass cases, and pale sculptures. Everything was neatly arranged, and their arrival seemed to have gone unnoticed.

Another trait is that Sloan is an extremely self-important gentleman, with many private matters; for example, there's a detailed - and tedious - description of an affair with a woman, but he carefully omits her name."

[1901] "Perhaps it was because he was thinking about his own imminent death that he put all his thoughts aside."

However, he didn't write anything, not a single word that night!"

[1910] Part Two
[1911] "Most of the great inventions of modern science have been due to inventors who have applied rigorous and relentless logic to a series of actions and reactions... " A simple explanation of pure lead 'burning' by Lavisse - which now seems quite simple to us - revealed a long-standing misconception about the unknown substance of phlogiston in the minds of people for centuries...
[1912] His conclusion, which now seems quite ordinary and unremarkable in our modern scientific context, was that since a substance weighed one ounce before burning in the air and weighed 0.07 ounces after burning in the air, it meant that some substance had been added to the original ore, thereby increasing its weight... It took humans 1600 years to grasp this point, and they named the new substance lead oxide.
[1913] "There is no criminal phenomenon that cannot be explained. Persistence and simple logic are the lifeline of the detective. What seems mysterious to the unthinking is a self-evident truth to the thoughtful... Detecting criminals is no longer a medieval practice of seeking oracles, but a highly precise branch of modern science. Its essence lies in logical reasoning."
[1914] - Quoted from Dr. George Ching-Chi-Kleff's "The Remnants of Modern Science" (147-8 pages)

[1916] Twenty-two
[1917] Ellery Queen became increasingly disheartened. Despite his vast knowledge of classic knowledge, he found that even Greek and Roman ancient philosophy could not help him. Ellery felt helpless. As the days passed, he was left with an ever-deepening sense of disappointment, with no joy in his heart, only bitterness and pain.
[1918] However, others had a lively time this week. Gilbert Sloan's suicide and burial were like a floodgate opening. Large newspapers covered it extensively, focusing on Gilbert Sloan's background.
[1919] They insulted the dead man relentlessly, without even bothering to refine their insults, resulting in contradictions and flaws, tarnishing Sloan's reputation and making him stink. This wave also swept over his family, with Théophane Sloan being the first to bear the brunt. Articles about the widow were also published.
[1920] A small newspaper, which could have been called "The Big Business Paper" but was not, proposed to the widow that they would pay her a large sum of money if she agreed to allow a group of articles to be published under her signature, with the title "The Autobiography of Théophane Sloan, Wife of a Murderer." Such a generous offer was met with a scornful look, and the newspaper, a shining example of the news industry's shamelessness, managed to dig up some private details of Théophane Sloan's first marriage and made them public, as if they had made a glorious discovery in the field of archaeology.
[1921] During this time, the police headquarters were relatively quiet. Officers resumed their routine duties, occasionally providing explanations for certain issues to complete the official report on the "Kafkas-Greenshaw-Sholans Case," as the case was known by the newspapers.
[1922] Dr. Broudy performed a thorough but sloppy autopsy on Gilbert Sholans' body, finding no signs of poisoning or violence; there were no bullet wounds, and the bullet wound in the sun was consistent with someone who had shot themselves. Consequently, the medical examiner's office allowed the return of Gilbert Sholans' body, which was buried in a cemetery with a beautiful grave surrounded by flowers.
[1923] In Ellery Queen's view, the only piece of information that could be reluctantly accepted was that Gilbert Sholans had died instantly. However, Ellery also admitted that, under the thick fog of confusion, he could not see how this fact could solve anything.
[1924] At that moment, in the midst of darkness, Ellery did not know that this fog would soon dissipate; the fact that Gilbert Sholans had died instantly would soon become a clear landmark.

[2102] [Note] Laissez faire: These two words are French, meaning "laissez faire" or "laissez-faire" in English, referring to the policy of non-interference in commercial affairs, especially by the government.
[2103] [Note] The original text reads "Here was to be enacted the Gethsemane of her marital life," where Gethsemane is a place in the Bible where Jesus was betrayed and captured. It is also known as the Garden of Gethsemane.
[2104] [Note] Mais oui: These two words are French, meaning "yes" or "indeed" in English.
[2105] [Note] Moses: Moses is a figure in the Bible who is said to have led the Hebrews out of slavery in Egypt and to have written much of the Jewish Torah and other religious texts.
[2106] [Note] ma lieutenante: These two words are French. It means "my lieutenant" in French.
[2107] Twenty-four: Evidence
[2108] On October 22nd, Friday, Mr. Ellery Queen - of course, informally - visited the mansion. That is, James Knox called on the phone, asking Mr. Queen to come to his residence immediately for a matter of interest. Mr. Queen readily agreed, not only because he admired the famous man but also because of a more practical reason. So he quickly hailed a beautiful taxi and drove down the riverfront, getting off in front of a magnificent building that commanded respect. He paid the suddenly deferential driver and then, with a steady gait, entered the mansion, which was considered one of the finest properties in the city, even in a city renowned for its real estate prices. He waited for a while in a reception room that seemed to have been transplanted from the Medici Palace, and then, without much ceremony, was led by a tall, thin, uniformed butler to see the host.
[2109] Despite the opulence of the surroundings, the host was engrossed in his work, bent over a very modern desk in his - the word was one that Mr. Queen had heard from the elderly, tall police officer's butler - in his "study." The study was as modern as the desk. Black lacquered walls, angular furniture, lamps that seemed to be straight out of a madman's dream... All the latest conveniences of a private office were present.
[2110] Additionally, there was Miss Joan Blayton, sitting primly next to the master, with a notebook on her lap.
[2111] Mr. Knox warmly welcomed Mr. Queen, offering him a wooden box filled with long cigarettes and inviting him to sit in a chair that appeared uncomfortable but was actually very comfortable. [2148] Ellery had a feeling that something was about to happen. Knox seemed only curious. Ellery opened the watch's back cover. A rolled-up piece of paper was inside, which unfolded to reveal a thousand-dollar bill.
[2149] Ellery was disappointed; the possibility he had imagined in Knox's study was shattered by this real bill. However, being a practical young man, he still compared the bill's number to the one in his pocket and found that it was one of the five Knox had claimed. He closed the watch cover and put it back in the archive materials.
[2150] "Quinn, what do you think of this?"
[2151] "Nothing earth-shattering," Elly replies glumly. "This new fact doesn't change the objective conditions on which they based their conclusion about Sloane. If Sloane killed Greenshaw, and Greenshaw's unknown accomplice was Sloane, then finding the bill still in the watchcase only means Sloane knew nothing about the bill. It means Greenshaw planned to swindle Kagist alone, never intending to tell Sloane about the thousand-dollar demand or to share it. The fact that he hid the bill so secretly shows this. [2544] Thirty: Interrogation
[2545] These people moved stealthily, in groups of two or three, under the cover of a moonless night, and without arousing suspicion, they all emerged from the servants' quarters at the Knox residence. [2572] Challenge the reader
[2573] Sincerely,
[2574] To the readers of this book:
[2575] As the plot of "The Mystery of the Greek Coffin" has developed to this point, I, as usual, insert a few words to test the reader's wisdom, which brings me great joy and satisfaction.
[2576] Why the joy? Because, among the cases I have been involved in, this one presents the most complex difficulties. It is indeed a pleasure—for a person who is often teased by bookstore customers with such remarks as, "Is this a real mystery?" They ask, "Oh, I solved it right away!"—to be able to reply with the following sentence:
[2577] "Well, readers, perhaps you think you've solved it perfectly, but you've been
[2578] duped!"
[2579] I may be overly optimistic. Nonetheless, the case is closed. Careful readers, you should know that you now have all the facts and can accurately answer this three-part question: Who strangled Yarpo Green Shayne, shot Gilbert Slane, and stole James Knox's paintings?
[2580] I humbly suggest: Cardeàvous, go ahead and puzzle over it!
[2581]——Eliot Quinlan
[2582][Note] Carde à vous: This French phrase translates to "You take care" or "You be careful." It's a political way of asking someone to be mindful or careful in a given situation
[2583] Thirty-One: Conclusion
[2584] Ellery added, "Mr. Knox, are you absolutely certain that the painting was stolen? Did you personally place it behind this panel?"
[2585] The expert's complexion had regained its color; he nodded slightly with a touch of reluctance: "I saw it just a week ago. It's here. No one else knows about it. Not a soul. This panel has been made for a long time."
[2586] "What I want to know," the detective said, "is how this could have happened. When was the painting stolen? If what Mr. Knox says is true, how did the thief know where it was?"
[2587] "The painting was not stolen tonight," the prosecutor said calmly. "So why didn't the alarm work?"
[2588] "Crafter said it was fine yesterday, and probably the day before," Pepper interjected.
[2589] Knox shrugged. Ellery said, "It will all come out in the wash. Please follow me back to Mr. Knox's study. Everyone, please come with me."
[2590] When they returned to the room with the leather walls, Ellery got to work. He closed the door, asked Pepper to stand guard, and then, without hesitation, he approached a low, floor-level iron grate on one of the walls. He fiddled with it for a moment, then pulled it out and placed it on the floor. He reached into the hole behind it. Everyone leaned forward to see. Inside was a large radiator. Ellery's fingers moved quickly along the pipes, as if playing a harp.
[2591] "Look here," he said, smiling, although it seemed to everyone that they could see nothing.
[2592] "There are eight pipes here, seven of them hot, and this one -" his hand stopped on the last pipe - "this one is cold." He bent over again, undoing some mechanism at the bottom of the cold pipe. Soon he had twisted off a fake cap, stood up, and held in his hand a long, thick pipe. "There we go," he said, smiling. "Very clever, Mr. Knox," he added, turning the pipe over. At the bottom was a barely noticeable metal wire. Ellery squeezed it, and the bottom moved, revealing that the pipe was lined with asbestos. He placed the cap on a chair, held up the pipe, and shook it vigorously. With his other hand, he caught something falling from the radiator pipe.
[2593] "What is it?" the officer whispered.
[2594] Ellery deftly unrolled the canvas. He flattened it out.
[2595] It was a painting - oil paint thick and heavy, depicting a medieval battle scene: a group of fierce warriors, fighting and killing each other, struggling over a fluttering banner.
[2596] "Believe it or not," Ellery said, spreading the painting on Knox's desk, "what you are looking at now is a million-dollar masterpiece, a rare treasure. In other words, this is the elusive Leonardo."
[2597] "Bullshit!" exclaimed James Knox, standing stiffly nearby, frowning and staring at the painting.
[2598] "Is that so, Mr. Knox? I discovered this great work of art when I interrupted your home this afternoon. You said it had been stolen from you, so how do you explain the fact that something that was supposedly in the hands of the thief is now in your study?"
[2599] "When I said 'bullshit,' I meant that you were 'bullshitting.' " Knox chuckled. "I thought I had underestimated your abilities, Mr. Quinn. However, you have made a fool of yourself. What I said was true: the Leonardo has been stolen. I'll tell you a fact: I have two copies of this painting -"
[2600] "Two?" the prosecutor gasped.
[2601] "Yes," Knox sighed. "I planned to keep a backup. What you see before you is the second copy – one that I've had for a long time. This painting could be by Lorenzo di Credi or one of his disciples, and my experts haven't been able to determine it. But it's not a genuine Leonardo. Lorenzo's imitation of Leonardo was remarkable, and his disciples also learned from their teacher. This work was likely made after the 1503 Florentine fresco project was abandoned, based on Leonardo's original. The Great Hall of the Medici Palace. And –"
[2602] "Let's not get into an art class, Mr. Knox," the police officer said. "What we want to know is –"
[2603] "So, your experts believe," Ellery said calmly, "that this painting was made after Leonardo's fresco was abandoned – I remember reading in art class that the main body was already painted, but it melted and peeled off when it got hot – and someone made this painting based on the main body of Leonardo's fresco. Is that right?"
[2604] "Yes. Anyway, the value of this second copy is only a fraction of the original Leonardo. That's obvious. When I bought the original from Kagisu – yes, I admit I bought a genuine one and believed it was genuine – I already owned this contemporary copy. I didn't mention it because I had plans... Well, if I was forced to return the original to the Victoria Museum, I would replace it with this worthless copy that I bought from Kagisu –"
[2605] "That's all we need to know, Mr. Knox," the police officer said. "We'll take this painting into evidence and continue our investigation."
[2606] Simpson's eyes lit up: "Now, we have enough human evidence, Mr. Knox. What about the original work?"
[2607] Knox insisted, "The original was stolen. I had it hidden in the small storeroom behind the paneling in my own study. And for heaven's sake, who would have thought—the copy, I had hidden in the disguised radiator pipe, and the thief clearly would not have known. But I'll tell you, the original was stolen! I don't know how he did it, but he did. And I admit, I intended to keep the original secret and use the copy to deceive the museum, but—"
[2608] The prosecutor pulled Ellery, the officer, and Pepper aside, whispering and conferring for a moment. Ellery listened intently, speaking with a certain amount of confidence, and they returned to Knox, who remained standing there, dumbfounded, by the desk with the oil paintings. As for Joan Britt, she leaned against the black leather wall, wide-eyed and motionless, her chest heaving with rapid breaths.
[2609] "No, sir," Ellery said, "I have to disagree. Both the prosecutor and Detective Queen believe - in this case, you should understand - they cannot accept your argument that this is a copy of Leonardo's work rather than the genuine article. We, the people present here, are not qualified to make an expert judgment. I believe we should listen to the opinion of the experts. May I -?"
[2610] Without waiting for Knox's hesitant nod, he stepped over to the telephone and dialed a number, briefly conversing with the other party before hanging up: "I just called Toby Johnson, who is arguably one of the leading art critics in the eastern United States, Mr. Knox. Do you know him?"
[2611] "I've met him," Knox replied briefly.
[2612] "He'll be here soon, Mr. Knox. In the meantime, we might as well take a break and rest our spirits."
[2613] Toby Johnson was a short, plump old man with piercing eyes and a well-dressed appearance, exuding an air of authority. After Craft led him in, he was promptly ushered out; Ellery, having spoken with him on the phone, introduced him to the others. Johnson was particularly friendly with Knox.
[2614] As he stood there waiting for someone to speak, his gaze was drawn to the painting on the desk.
[2615] Ellery spoke up first: "This is a serious matter, Mr. Johnson," he began calmly, "so I must insist that everything discussed in this room tonight remains confidential."
[2616] Johnson nodded, as if he had heard this request before.
[2617] "Very well, sir," Ellery gestured towards the painting, "Mr. Johnson, can you identify the author of this oil painting?"
[2618] The room fell silent as the expert smiled, put on a magnifying glass, and stepped closer to the desk. He carefully laid the painting on the ground, flattened it, and examined it closely; then, he instructed Ellery and Pepper to each take one side and hold it up in the air while he directed the soft light of several lamps onto the painting.
[2619] No one spoke, and Johnson worked in silence. His round face showed no expression.
[2620] He examined the painting inch by inch, focusing particularly on the faces of the figures closest to the flag... After working for half an hour, he nodded happily, and Ellery and Pepper returned the painting to the desk. Knox let out a slow breath; his eyes remained fixed on the expert's face.
[2621] "This painting has a history," Johnson finally spoke. "And it's closely related to what I'm about to tell you." Everyone listened intently to his every word. "It has been known for years, actually for centuries, that there are two paintings in this series, identical except for one thing…"
[2622] Someone murmured.
[2623] "Identical, except for one thing. One painting, known to be by Leonardo da Vinci, was commissioned by Piero Soderini to decorate a wall in his palace's new council chamber in Florence. Leonardo chose as his subject the battle of the Florentine Republic's generals against Niccolò Piccinino near the Ponte Vecchio in 1440. The original sketch, technically known as a maquette, was actually called The Battle of Anghiari. This was a great fresco project, and coincidentally, Michelangelo also participated, choosing a subject from the Battle of Pisa. However, Leonardo was unable to complete the work, and Mr. Knox may know this; he stopped after completing the sketch for the battle of the standard. The paint peeled off the wall after being heated, and the work was lost.
[2624] "Leonardo left Florence. It is generally assumed that he was disappointed with his wasted effort and made an oil painting from the original sketch as a self-artistic evaluation. Well, there are many legends about this painting, but unfortunately, it was 'lost' until a few years ago, when a field worker from the Victoria and Albert Museum in London discovered it in Italy."
[2625] Everyone was entranced, utterly silent, yet Johnson seemed oblivious.
[2626] "Later," he went on, "there were many copies of the sketch, and the works of young Raphael, Fra Bartoloméo, and others were very famous, but the sketch itself was damaged after being used as a model for these copies. The sketch was lost; the original fresco on the wall was covered by a new fresco by Vasari in 1560. Therefore, the discovery of Leonardo's original work – to put it mildly – was a monumental discovery in the art world. This led to a fascinating story.
[2627] "I've already mentioned that there are two such paintings, and they are identical in every respect except for one thing. The first painting was discovered a long time ago and exhibited; its authorship has never been conclusively established until about six years ago when the Victoria and Albert Museum made a significant discovery. The problem lies here. Experts have never been able to determine whether the first discovered painting is by Leonardo or not; in fact, it is generally believed to be the work of Lorenzo di Credi or one of his pupils. As with all disputes in the art world, there have been much joking, anger, and slander; it was only six years ago that the painting was discovered by the Victoria and Albert Museum that this case was finally settled.
[2628] "There are some ancient records that suggest that there are two paintings of this subject: one by Leonardo and one by a copyist – the author of the copy is not clearly stated in these records. It is said that the two paintings are identical except for one thing: the skin tone of the figure standing near the flag, there is a slight difference. According to legend, the skin tone in Leonardo's painting is slightly darker – a very small difference, so the records always say that only when the two paintings are placed side by side can you be sure which is Leonardo's original. So, you see..."
[2629] "Interesting," Ellery murmured, "Mr. Knox, did you know this before?"
[2630] "Of course. Cardox knew it too." Knox nodded his head, "I've already said that I've owned this painting, so when Cardox sold me the other one, I could easily compare the two paintings to determine which one was Leonardo's original. And now..." He sighed, "The Leonardo original is missing."
[2631] "Oh?" Johnstone looked uneasy. But he smiled again. "Well, it doesn't concern me. Anyway, the two paintings were kept in the museum for a long time, so the museum could easily determine that the painting found by its field workers was Leonardo's original. Later, the other one, the copy, disappeared. Rumor has it that it was sold to an American millionaire collector, who paid a huge sum, even though it was known to be a copy."
[2632] Johnstone straightened his thin chest: "One day, if the Leonardo's work in the museum also disappears, it will be very difficult - I should say impossible - to judge whether it is an original based solely on one painting. You can only judge it by one painting, and you can never be sure..."
[2633] "Mr. Johnstone, what about this one?" Ellery asked.
[2634] "This one," Johnstone shrugged, "is certainly one of the two, but without the other one for comparison -" he stopped, tapping his forehead, "of course! I'm stupid. This must be the copy. The original is far away, in the Victoria Museum."
[2635] "Yes, yes. That's right," Ellery said quickly. "Since the two paintings are so similar, Mr. Johnstone, why is one worth a million and the other only a few thousand?"
[2636] "Yes, yes," Ellery quickly said, "since the two are so similar, Johnstone, why is one worth a million and the other only a few thousand?"
[2637] "My dear sir!" the expert exclaimed, "It's - what can I say? - so naive a question. What's the difference between an original Rembrandt and a modern copy? Leonardo was a master; and the author of the copy, according to legend, was probably a disciple of Lorraine, just copying Leonardo's work. The difference in value is like the difference between a masterpiece and a beginner's sketch. Even if the copy mimics Leonardo's style perfectly, what does that matter? Mr. Quin, you wouldn't say that a forged signature of yours, made by a photograph, is equal to your own signature, would you?"
[2638] Johnstone seemed to be getting carried away, so Ellery politely thanked him and sent him out the door. The expert didn't calm down until he left, and everyone regained their composure.
[2639] "Art! Leonardo!" the officer said with a disgusted tone, "Now it's even more tangled than before. My police career is over."
[2640] "Actually, things aren't that bad," the prosecutor said thoughtfully, "Johnstone's explanation at least confirms Knox's statement that even if no one can tell which is which. At least now we know there are two paintings, and we thought there was only one. So - we must find the thief who stole the other one."
[2641] "I don't understand," said Pepper. "Why didn't the museum mention the second painting? After all -"
[2642] "My dear Pepper," said Ellery calmly, "they have the original. Why would they care about a copy? They have no interest in the copy... yes, Simpson, you're absolutely right. The person we're investigating is the one who stole the other painting, the one who wrote the threatening letter to Knox, the one who used the check as stationery, so he must also be the one who framed Sholander and killed Greenshaw and framed George Kastel."
[2643] "What a brilliant summary," sneered Simpson. "Since you've put together everything we know, why not tell us what we don't know? Who is this person?"
[2644] Ellery sighed. "Simpson, Simpson, you always try to make me look bad, to embarrass me... Do you really want to know who this person is?"
[2645] Simpson's eyes widened, and the detective began to take an interest. "Do I really want to know? He actually asks me that!" The prosecutor pulled
[2646] His gaze sharpened, he paused. "I think, Ellery," he said calmly, "you don't really know either, do you?"
[2647] "Yes," Knox said. "Quinn, who is it?"
[2648] Ellery smiled. "I'm glad you asked that question, Mr. Knox. You must have read this phrase while reading, because many famous people - La Fontaine, Dunsinane, Coleridge, Cicero, Juvenal, Diogenes - have already repeated it in various ways. This phrase is also the motto of the Temple of Apollo in Tylus, attributed to Thales, Pythagoras, and Solon. In Latin, it is: Ne quis nimis. In English, it means: "You should know yourself." James Knox, Esq.," Ellery said in a voice that was imbued with human warmth, "you are under arrest!"

[2668] "Furthermore, after examining the typewritten threatening letters, what did we find?
[2669] "Well, the first threatening letter was typed on an 'Endwood' typewriter, coincidentally, the anonymous letter reporting that Mrs. Sloan and Greenshaw were brothers used the same typewriter. [2670] "And what was the clue we discovered?
[2671] He turned around and quickly wrote the following image on the blackboard with chalk: $30,000.
[2672] "Now, everyone, please look," Ellery turned back and said, "The typist's mistake was that he did not completely release the 'character change button' after typing the dollar sign ($), resulting in a half-broken character appearing on the paper when he pressed the second key – that is, when he typed the '3' key.
[2673] "The typist naturally pressed the 'reverse button' to retype the '3', but this is irrelevant; what matters is that the half-broken '3' character still exists on the paper. So, what happened after this common typing mistake –
[2674] "The mistake is this: when attempting to print the character on the lower row of the keyboard, the 'character change button' or 'uppercase font button' was not completely released, resulting in the upper part of the lower row character being printed above the space.
[2675] "So, let's see, I drew a sketch on the blackboard. Can you understand it this way?"
[2676] Many people nodded.
[2677] "Great," Ellery continued, "let's think about how the '3' key on a standard keyboard looks like on all typewriters, referring to the American typewriter. What does it look like? The '3' is on the lower row of the keyboard, and the upper row represents the symbols for numbers. Let me show you."
[2678] He turned around again and wrote the following symbol on the blackboard with chalk: #.
[2679] "Simple, right?" He turned around and said, "But I want you to notice that the mistake in the second threatening letter indicates that it was not typed on a standard keyboard, at least the '3' key is not standard. Because, the broken character above the position where the '3' is printed in the second threatening letter should be the lower half of the '#' symbol, but - as you can see on the blackboard - it's not the case! Instead, it's a very special symbol -
[2680] A circle on the left, dragging a curve to the right."
[2681] He stood upright and calmly: "Please think for a moment. Look at the symbol I sketched above the '3' on the blackboard."
[2682] Everyone stared, but no one spoke. "It's really quite obvious," Ellery finally said. "I'm surprised that none of you - especially the journalists - have figured it out. I can confidently say that I'm the only one who can argue against this - I believe that the 'circle curve' can only be the lower half of a symbol that could appear on a typewriter - a symbol that looks like a capital letter 'L' with a small horizontal line in the middle - in other words, the symbol for the British pound:
[2683] (£)"
[2684] There was a murmur of astonishment and admiration.
[2685] "Good, well. We just need to find a 'Remington' typewriter - of course, an American one - with the '#' symbol on the upper row of the '3' key. From a probability standpoint, an American 'Remington' typewriter would have this foreign symbol on this key - I believe the probability is one in a million. In other words, if you can find a typewriter with this symbol on this key, I can guarantee, based on mathematics and logic, that it is the typewriter used for the second threatening letter."
[2686] "This is basic logic. All I needed to do was to establish one more fact to complete my reasoning; if I could determine the position of the '#' symbol that had been replaced by the symbol for the British pound sterling (£) on the key that had been exchanged, I could unequivocally state that the 'Remington' typewriter used to type the second threat letter was indeed Knox's."
[2687] "This is a given fact, and the evidence is clear: the 'Remington' typewriter used to type the second threat letter was James Knox's."
